    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_591985716" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/591985716" />
    <a title="John Alt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/18585310-john-alt"><img alt="John Alt" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/591985716?book_show_action=true">Apr 16, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="John Alt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="John Alt" href="/user/show/18585310-john-alt">John Alt</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer591985716" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6360116724314291815">When James Jones died the Army lost one of its own. Here was a soldier, a man with an abiding regard for things military. Many novelists treat war and the Army but only with a passing interest. They write one book and get it out of their systems. For Jones, <i>From Here to Eternity</i> was the start of a lifelong study of what it means to be a soldier. To the day he died he thought like a soldier. Other writers delve into high society or family life or <i>la vie boheme</i>. Jones was at his best when he explo</span>
  <span id="freeText6360116724314291815" style="display:none">When James Jones died the Army lost one of its own. Here was a soldier, a man with an abiding regard for things military. Many novelists treat war and the Army but only with a passing interest. They write one book and get it out of their systems. For Jones, <i>From Here to Eternity</i> was the start of a lifelong study of what it means to be a soldier. To the day he died he thought like a soldier. Other writers delve into high society or family life or <i>la vie boheme</i>. Jones was at his best when he explored the mind of an enlisted man. He was not career Army but he made the Army his career. It had its name on everything he wrote.<br /><br />The Pineapple Army was what he knew best. The modern, relatively democratized Army was alien to him. He would appreciate the tremendous improvements in the life of the enlisted man. We must look across decades to imagine what his life was like. He himself was an EM. Then, enlisted men were poorly paid, harshly disciplined, often humiliated, and rarely listened to. But the time was the Great Depression and opportunity outside was nil. The Army gave a man three square meals a day and a roof over his head. Jones, like so many others, was a refugee from the bread line.<br /><br />Enlisting in 1939, he left a stormy home life. He could not stand his mother; his father committed suicide. He was at Hickam Field in Honolulu on December 7, 1941. <i>From Here to Eternity</i> contains a memorable account of that day: it is a lazy Sunday morning breakfast until the mess dishes begin to rattle. The men wonder why gunnery practice is so close and so early. The calm sweeps into frenzy. The barracks are raked by diving Zeros. Men running across the quadrant are stitched in their tracks as machine gun bullets walk over them, kicking up dust. Jones survived the Sunday Morning Massacre only to get his in the South Pacific.<br /><br />He was a belly-in-the-mud jungle fighter on Guadalcanal. <i>The Thin Red Line</i> is based on that combat. He vividly describes the white-hot bloody battles for hills with numbers. History has forgotten the numbers. Jones never forgot the courage and the cowardice of men under fire. The novel is almost a textbook of battle. It has remarkably close and accurate descriptions of men at war. Couple this with a precise memory, a keen eye for detail, an acute ear for soldier-speak, and a first-rate style emerges. Most of all, he depicts battle with the intense vision of a man striving to be objective. All is in perspective; no one is blamed. The weaknesses and strengths of men are facts to be accepted.<br /><br />Jones’ men become soldiers only as they face up to death. Seeing dead men all around, they must realize that a bullet will catch up with them, if not on this campaign, then on the next island. If not on the next, then the one after, for the war will last longer than their luck will. Physical agony is all around in <i>The Thin Red Line</i>. A man ripped open, clutching his purple intestines spilling into the dirt beneath him, screams for help, while slowly, too slowly, he dies. Another man, going days without rest, under continual fire, about to break, receives a dear john. His wife tells him that she wants to marry a man she has been bedding.<br /><br />These men, doomed men, are the stuff of Jones’ stories. They are often the outcasts of society but they find dignity on the battlefield. Jones had faith that the average joe will come through, spirit intact. In <i>From Here to Eternity</i> Robert E. Lee Prewitt, or Prew, has left the coal mines of Harlan County, Kentucky. Like so many others during the Depression—and like Jones—he joins the Regular Army. While others wait for the job market to open, he finds his place in the Army. He can find nothing prouder than to be a Thirty Year Man. The Army gives him his dignity. He proves himself a good boxer and a superb bugilist. After a punch blinded his opponent, he turns from boxing to the sense of beauty he can find in the mournful notes of Taps. He finds a calling and is thankful to the Army that his life has purpose. Like his childhood guitar-playing, the bugle makes him feel that “pain might not be pointless” if it can be turned into music. Pain is indeed his lot. Jones shows that the same Army, the Pineapple Army, which gives Prew his music, is pitted against him.<br /><br />The Army becomes symbolic of the machinery of Jones’ 1930s society, which grinds individuals into cogs. Prew must assert his individual worth against social pressure. He refuses to box because he blinded a buddy, Dixie Wells, and he will not bugle because he will not play politics. So he transfers to an infantry outfit. There, he gets “the treatment.” The company commander is also regimental boxing coach. The noncoms, all boxers, relentlessly torment him to join the boxing team. Prew won’t break. <br /><br />He will not play the game. The novel also has another Thirty Year Man, a decent type who successfully plays the game. First Sergeant Milt Warden and Prewitt understand one another, even warily respect one another, but they can never agree. Warden calls Prewitt a hardhead. To Prewitt the matter is simple. “He had to leave the Bugle Corps because he was a bugler.” Red, a buddy, “did not have to leave it. But he had to leave because most of all he wanted to stay.” This is Prewitt’s kind of integrity. If you do something well, he believes, then you must give it your utmost. There can be no compromise. If politics force compromise, then get out of the Bugle Corps.<br /><br />Jones himself chose an unconventional life. After recovery time in military hospitals he was mustered out in 1944. But he took the Army home with him and, like Prewitt, remained a Thirty Year Man for the rest of his life. He chose a hard road in civilian life. While in the hospital he decided to become a writer. His first novel was rejected by Scribners in 1945. The next six years were impoverished and difficult until <i>From Here to Eternity</i>. It became an immediate success in 1951. Established as a writer, and royalties flowing in, he wrote other novels, including <i>Some Came Running</i> and <i>The Merry Month of May</i>.<br /><br />Prewitt played the bugle. Jones wrote his books. Jones sang of valor and tragedy, comradeship and hatred, barbarity and kindness. He pulled no punches. His characters are without halos. Some of them perform unspeakable cruelties in the name of civilization. Nonetheless, Jones shows these men as occasionally noble, heroic, self-sacrificing. They are real-life men, or composites of them—soldiers he fought with, ate with, drank with.<br /><br />He is gone. His words remain. They are a gift from him to us. He had a poem by Yeats read at his graveside. The poem, “Sailing to Byzantium,” has some telling lines in it: “That is no country for old men. The young/ in one another’s arms, birds in the trees,/ . . . fish, flesh, and fowl, commend all summer long,/ whatever is begotten, born, and dies . . . And therefore I have sailed the seas and come/ To the holy city of Byzantium.” He has crossed the seas. He wrote much about what men needed for the voyage. They need bravery and dignity. And so he is in Byzantium and we still have our summers and birds in the trees. Now his stories belong to soldiers living and soldiers yet to be born. Hence, he will live on. So long as there is an Army there will always be a James Jones.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6360116724314291815" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_411338676" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/411338676" />
    <a title="Julie G" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5688280-julie-g"><img alt="Julie G" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1308962845p2/5688280.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/411338676?book_show_action=true">Sep 10, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Julie G" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Julie G" href="/user/show/5688280-julie-g">Julie G</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="ebook 9781453215111" href="/book/show/11066930-from-here-to-eternity">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5688280-julie-g?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer411338676" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5584791892129463442">Originally published by Scribner in 1951, James Jones' novel was heavily edited to, purportedly, get it past the censors of the time. To present a more tasteful image of life in the military. Now, thanks to Jones' family and OpenRoad Media, we can read the book as it was written.<br/><br/>In the wake of the Depression, military service was the only option for many young men in America. Men who were poor, poorly educated, or poor of spirit had few choices in the early 20th century.<br/><br/>On an Army base in Hawaii</span>
  <span id="freeText5584791892129463442" style="display:none">Originally published by Scribner in 1951, James Jones' novel was heavily edited to, purportedly, get it past the censors of the time. To present a more tasteful image of life in the military. Now, thanks to Jones' family and OpenRoad Media, we can read the book as it was written.<br /><br />In the wake of the Depression, military service was the only option for many young men in America. Men who were poor, poorly educated, or poor of spirit had few choices in the early 20th century.<br /><br />On an Army base in Hawaii, in the early weeks of 1941, Robert E Lee 'Prew' Prewitt is a helluva fighter and the "best bugler in the Regment [sic]." Although only twenty-one, he had lived 'on the bum' for years. Seeking to improve his lot in life, Prew chose The Profession.<br /><br />At his first post with the 27th, Prew became a boxer. After a bout that nearly killed a man, he gave up fighting. Constant harassment and abuse, designed to force him back in the ring, instead sent Prew to 'A' Company, home of the bugle corps.<br /><br />Now, as the novel begins, Prew has been passed over for promotion to First Bugler in favor of a company 'pet.' There are rumors that Prew rejected his commander's advances; he isn't saying. But, once again, he is transferred.<br /><br />His new home, 'G' Company, is regular infantry with a commander more focused on boxing than war. Since Prew refuses to fight, conflict is inevitable. And, with the help of his second-in-command, Captain Dana E. 'Dynamite' Holmes is determined to teach Prew the error of his ways.<br /><br />What follows is a portrait of military life on Hawaii in the months leading up to Pearl Harbor, and the shocked and shocking days that came after. A portrait of men just trying to survive the politics, the discrimination, and the brutality of the few who held power over the many. Written by a man who lived it.<br /><br />*****<br /><br />I have never read the 1951 version of this novel and it's been many, many years since I saw the 1953 movie. Therefore, when I chose to read the restored edition, I had a vague Army-on-Hawaii-before-Pearl expectation of the book's content. Which is a bit like saying Moby Dick is about a guy and a big fish.<br /><br />What grabbed me, and stays with me as I write this, is the language. Not the F-bombs and C-word, expunged in the 50s and common today, but the way that language was used sixty years ago. Language molded in the mind of a remarkable writer.<br /><br />(A brilliant example can be found - here - at the James Jones Literary Society site.)<br /><br />It would take days, and skills I simply lack, to describe even a portion of this work. There are people and places that you can see, and hear, and smell, and feel. The voices and lives of Schofield Barracks will live with you long after you close your e-reader.<br /><br />Why not carve out a couple of weeks, pick up the ebook, and lose yourself?<br /><br /> <br />Disclosure of Material Connection: I received a complimentary electronic galley of this book from the publisher through NetGalley.com  professional readers program. I was not required to write a positive review. The opinions I have expressed are my own.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5584791892129463442" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2116064264" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2116064264" />
    <a title="Steven Meyers" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/71163840-steven-meyers"><img alt="Steven Meyers" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1504728076p2/71163840.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2116064264?book_show_action=true">Sep 06, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Steven Meyers" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Steven Meyers" href="/user/show/71163840-steven-meyers">Steven Meyers</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2116064264" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12391387940108448898">Many veterans who have read Mr. Jones's novel assert it was an accurate portrayal of the times in the Army. I'll take their word for it. The closest I ever came to serving in the military was joining the Cub Scouts and playing with my G.I. Joe action figure (a.k.a. doll) when I was a kid. The novel revolves two major characters, Robert E. Lee Prewitt and Milton Anthony Warden. There are other notable individuals such as Angelo Maggio, Dana Holmes, and the cook Maylon Stark. The two major women c</span>
  <span id="freeText12391387940108448898" style="display:none">Many veterans who have read Mr. Jones's novel assert it was an accurate portrayal of the times in the Army. I'll take their word for it. The closest I ever came to serving in the military was joining the Cub Scouts and playing with my G.I. Joe action figure (a.k.a. doll) when I was a kid. The novel revolves two major characters, Robert E. Lee Prewitt and Milton Anthony Warden. There are other notable individuals such as Angelo Maggio, Dana Holmes, and the cook Maylon Stark. The two major women characters Karen Holmes and Alma "Lorene" Schmidt are well developed but take a back seat to Prewitt's and Warden's stories. 'From Here to Eternity' exudes masculinity in all its glory, violence, sexuality, and imbecility. It is a story about men obsessed with manliness and status. Petty politics reins supreme. The government-sanction frat club continually fight boredom and sexual frustration during peace time. I had to keep reminding myself that most of them were young immature men.<br /><br />The novel was published in 1951. It was understandable that the publishers originally edited out some of the more unseemly material because of the American market's more puritanical sensibilities. Fortunately, 'From Here to Eternity' has been restored to the author's original intent. The racy material would not even meet up to the standard of erotica in today's society. Maybe it got the blood rushing and heart pounding while reading it in the 1950s but today it's meh. What I did find shocking, considering when it was published, is the laissez-faire attitude towards homosexuality. It sometimes is depicted as predatory and other times as consensual. Also, be warned, racist jargon is peppered throughout the work. African-Americans, Jews, and Italians are especially targeted. Also, alcohol was apparently one of the major food groups.<br /><br />It is a brutal book but not gloomy. 'From Here to Eternity' is loaded with irony and thoughtful discussions about a variety of human conditions. It occurs during a time when women had limited options and Jim Crow was still very much alive. Information was easily manipulated for patriotic reasons and military culture was insular. Mr. Jones's impressive novel is to be savored. It is not some swaggering John Wayne-like comic book but a grown-up's work. For nearly three decades, I avoided reading 'From Here to Eternity' because of the movie poster famously showing Burt Lancaster and Deborah Kerr sucking face on the beach. I've never seen the film and assumed the book was some kind of military Harlequin Romance drivel. Boy, was I ever wrong.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12391387940108448898" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_64692984" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/64692984" />
    <a title="Lee Anne" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/966521-lee-anne"><img alt="Lee Anne" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1204751883p2/966521.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/64692984?book_show_action=true">Jul 23, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lee Anne" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lee Anne" href="/user/show/966521-lee-anne">Lee Anne</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover " href="/book/show/6640698-from-here-to-eternity">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer64692984" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10216660505993321757">There are apparently three options for the Army men serving in Hawaii in the days just before Pearl Harbor: get an island girl (or some other Asian or Pacific Islander) in a shack; visit one of the many, many local whorehouses (if you have $15, apparently you can even go "around the world"); or get liquored up courtesy of a wealthy, gay sugardaddy. That was a scene you didn't see closeted Montgomery Clift and Frank m-f-ing Sinatra play in the movie version. And it was one of the many surprises i</span>
  <span id="freeText10216660505993321757" style="display:none">There are apparently three options for the Army men serving in Hawaii in the days just before Pearl Harbor: get an island girl (or some other Asian or Pacific Islander) in a shack; visit one of the many, many local whorehouses (if you have $15, apparently you can even go "around the world"); or get liquored up courtesy of a wealthy, gay sugardaddy. That was a scene you didn't see closeted Montgomery Clift and Frank m-f-ing Sinatra play in the movie version. And it was one of the many surprises in this epic book. <br /><br />The reality of otherwise straight men possibly using homosexual men for cash (maybe or maybe not in exchange for sexual favors) is treated shockingly rationally for a sixty year old book. There is a fair amount of homophobic teasing (the word "queer" is thrown around a lot), but none of the men in the unit who partake are shunned, or beaten--it's looked on as a natural consequence of their circumstances. And the two gay civilians are portrayed not as cariactures, but as real, feeling people. This really floored me, in light of "don't ask, don't tell" and how far we've come in gay rights just in the past ten years, to see someone in WWII be so frank about what was sooooo taboo then.<br /><br />I don't want to do a dis-service to this book, though, by focusing on what is really just one small part. If you're a fan of the movie, you'll find it was pretty faithful to the book in plot, but the book is so much more. Gritty, profane, dirty, blunt, sentimental, funny, violent, sad.<br /><br />This is a boy book (or a man book, I should say. Don Draper would totally have read this.), but full of internal thought and emotion. It's very philosophical in tone, too, and it makes me see why a philosophical filmmaker such as Terrence Malick chose to make another of Jones' books into a movie. <br /><br />I'm excited to recommend this to customers, as I often am when I find an older title that people may have heard of but never read.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10216660505993321757" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1253504254" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1253504254" />
    <a title="Doug" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/25667717-doug"><img alt="Doug" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605117426p2/25667717.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1253504254?book_show_action=true">Apr 12, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Doug" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Doug" href="/user/show/25667717-doug">Doug</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780812984316" href="/book/show/15796744-from-here-to-eternity">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1253504254" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14783763564140882891">My impetus for reading this came via an odd, circuitous route - I was listening to the recently shuttered, flop London musical based on the novel, and read that it, in turn, incorporated material from the uncensored, restored version of the book that had only been published a few years previously. I was intrigued that, among many other emendations, a lot of material about gay activities in the peacetime army had been excised. Not really remembering a lot from the award-winning film (or the subse</span>
  <span id="freeText14783763564140882891" style="display:none">My impetus for reading this came via an odd, circuitous route - I was listening to the recently shuttered, flop London musical based on the novel, and read that it, in turn, incorporated material from the uncensored, restored version of the book that had only been published a few years previously. I was intrigued that, among many other emendations, a lot of material about gay activities in the peacetime army had been excised. Not really remembering a lot from the award-winning film (or the subsequent TV miniseries of 1979), I decided to give it a try anyway ... and surprisingly, really, REALLY enjoyed reading it. Sure, there are languid passages of philosophical diatribes that seem anachronistically out of place - and stylistically atrocious, crazy-making things, like Jones' eschewing apostrophes, and stringing together 2 or 3 adverbs, willy-nilly. But he makes up for this with a genuinely exciting plot and characters that one really cares about. It is sad few people read Jones nowadays - he was an American original, and this National Book Award winning novel should be rediscovered, especially in this uncensored version, as Jones originally intended it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14783763564140882891" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/146142687?book_show_action=true">Feb 05, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rachel" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rachel" href="/user/show/3734253-rachel">Rachel</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780385333641" href="/book/show/789390.From_Here_to_Eternity">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer146142687" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10272560539623431151">Please re-title as "Reading for Eternity".<br/><br/>This book is WAY too long. My version was 852 pages. And I disliked most of them. I could have read it in far less than the 5 months it actually took me, if I hadn't kept putting it down and picking up more interesting books.<br/><br/>James Jones can be a very good writer, but not as good as he thought (or others thought) he was. I hated the stream of consciousness portions with sentences that went on for inches and paragraphs that were almost the length of entire</span>
  <span id="freeText10272560539623431151" style="display:none">Please re-title as "Reading for Eternity".<br /><br />This book is WAY too long. My version was 852 pages. And I disliked most of them. I could have read it in far less than the 5 months it actually took me, if I hadn't kept putting it down and picking up more interesting books.<br /><br />James Jones can be a very good writer, but not as good as he thought (or others thought) he was. I hated the stream of consciousness portions with sentences that went on for inches and paragraphs that were almost the length of entire pages. I hated the blatent overuse of adverbs, "she had very wide eyes that looked at him serenely openly." I never really liked any of the characters. Prewitt is full of misguided and (IMHO) unsubstantiated pride that causes him to always make the worst possible decisions. Warden is perhaps the most sympathetic character, except when he is with the woman he supposedly loves. Jones seems to think that love cannot exist without contempt (romantic love, love for a buddy, love for the army, love for oneself...) And that is perhaps why I really resisted reading this book. Jones' writes a very different philosophy of life than the one that I subscribe to. I think I would rather re-watch the movie (Burt Lancaster was a HOTTIE) than ever pick up this book again.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10272560539623431151" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_241567449" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/241567449" />
    <a title="Chris Gager" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5295008-chris-gager"><img alt="Chris Gager" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1307204529p2/5295008.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/241567449?book_show_action=true">Nov 30, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chris Gager" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chris Gager" href="/user/show/5295008-chris-gager">Chris Gager</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780451056245" href="/book/show/2217752.From_Here_to_Eternity">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer241567449" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer788524668918577258">Another long ago probable read overwhelmed by the memory of the movie. I might not have read all of it but recall that there were some major changes in the movie script. Mainly to soften some of JJ's more realistic edges. The later TV miniseries was truer to the book and Natalie Wood was a better choice than Deborah Kerr. Steve Railsback played Prewitt as recall. Date read is a guess.<br/><br/>As mentioned above, I may well have NOT read this back in the day. My memory is all geared to both the movie and </span>
  <span id="freeText788524668918577258" style="display:none">Another long ago probable read overwhelmed by the memory of the movie. I might not have read all of it but recall that there were some major changes in the movie script. Mainly to soften some of JJ's more realistic edges. The later TV miniseries was truer to the book and Natalie Wood was a better choice than Deborah Kerr. Steve Railsback played Prewitt as recall. Date read is a guess.<br /><br />As mentioned above, I may well have NOT read this back in the day. My memory is all geared to both the movie and TV versions of the story. So ... now I'm reading it for sure. So far it's taking a bit of patience and focus to adjust to the writing style of JJ. It seems just a BIT ripe to me. Still readable, however. One of the cool things about reading this is that I was on/in/at Schofield Barracks(which we just called "Schofield") many times during the almost-two years I was stationed on Oahu. My Navy base was just across the valley on the other side of Wahiawa. I was there in 68-69, just 27 years later. Not that much, really.<br /><br />The end of the story approaches and events unfold in a different way than in the movie. Violent, ugly stuff is going on and Pearl Harbor hasn't even happened yet. That's the HUGE irony hanging over the characters in the book. All this blood, sweat, toil and tears - and for what? Notes ...<br /><br />- Angelo = Orr in Catch-22<br /><br />- The author loves the Wobblies<br /><br />- The author's politics get clearer through the Malloy story, the telling of which brings the proceedings to a temporary halt.<br /><br />- Joe Hill was not to be found in the Columbia Illustrated Encylopedia.<br /><br />- the knife fight = Cities of the Plain<br /><br />- lots of "you see"-ing going on here - to be expected I suppose<br /><br />I'm soldiering on, wading through the pretentious over-writing in search of the story. It's there, of course, and worth following so far. JJ seems much in line with other wordy blowhards of the mid-20th century: Hemingway, Mailer, Sinclair Lewis, John Steinbeck, Thornton Wilder etc. They're ALL worth reading, but the style seems dated.<br /><br />Hanging in there despite the over-writing. Mr. Jones' pen occasionally wanders into a well-written/well observed bit, as in the scene where the broke and lonely-horny Prewitt wanders into the company day-room and cruises the magazines looking for and at pictures of attractive women. Been there ... done that. Even in my day, military pay was pathetically low and "normal" women kind of scarce.<br /><br />Last night I was all in on this book as I set Bill Finnegan's surfing saga down for a while. This one continues to be a bit of a challenge. Like walking in shoe(word)-sucking mud. If only ... if only ... Mr. Jones had been more economical and less enamored of his own thoughts and observations. His nicely wrought scene of Prewitt's "Taps" is nearly spoiled by the reminder about enlisted men on trash detail having to haul away the bloodied menstrual rags of the officer's wives. David Foster Wallace's lament about John Updike("did the ______ ever/never have an unexpressed thought?!?") applies to this book and author as well. Next up: Book Three - The Women ...<br /><br />Well, FINALLY! after nearly 300 pages the main issue of this book, Prewitt vs. the U. S. Army, is coming into focus. The mysterious, opaque character of Milt Warden is an important but secondary thing. Both of these Army gents may embody aspects of the author's self-perception. Prewitt loves the Army and wants to spend his life there. The Army doesn't care, and definitely doesn't love him back. It obviously has no room for Prewitt's kind of self-expression(artistic/sensitive). He doesn't really know what to do. I've seen the movie so I assume I know the outcome, though I understand that book-Maggio has a different fate than movie-Maggio(Sinatra). We'll see. I compare James Jones' writing to both Tolstoy and Dostoevsky. Like Tolstoy he wants to tell us everything, but unlike Dostoevski he uses way too many words to do it. Nevertheless I find myself getting more interested and comfortable in the story despite the over-writing. 500 pages to go ...<br /><br />- BTW, the haircuts of the army guys pictured on the cover of this paperback are NOT regulation. The same thing occurred in the film "Coming Home." Bruce Dern played a Marine Corps officer with all kinds of hair curling from under his cap down towards his collar. No Way!<br /><br />Movin' on as Prewitt will get to enjoy some of the good life before things get dicey for him. It's interesting that I've turned to two Hawaii located books recently. The other is William Finnegan's excellent memoir "Barbarian Days." Prewitt is shacking up in close proximity to where Finnegan's family lived 26 years later. I lived by the Ala Wai canal. The story is still entertaining me and It helped for me to look at some pictures of Hotel St. back in the day. When I was stationed on Oahu we were told to avoid Hotel St. so I never went there. Waikiki is a ways away from downtown Honolulu. I went there quite a bit.<br /><br />- Casting comparison: Joe Pantoliano played Maggio in the TV mini-series and in my opinion is a much closer fit to the book-Maggio than Sinatra.<br /><br />Last night ... after his big fight with Bloom, Prewitt goes to the beer-garden and chats with Jimmy the Kanaka bartender, a prizefighter. They talk about fighting at the Honolulu Civic Auditorium("the Civic") ... Center ... that's where I saw Led Zeppelin in the summer of 1969. Me and about a hundred other people - amazing! It's probably been torn down by now.<br /><br />And finally, after 500+ pages of set-up, we get to the "real" thing, the stockade stuff. Prewitt's in there(joining Maggio) and it ain't pretty. Someone's died already(not in the stockade) and more will follow. In a note at the beginning of the book Jones tells us that the stockade stuff did NOT happen at Schofield, but that it did happen at another Army base and that he had personal experience of it. Good enough for me. I was never in the brig while in the Navy and most definitely wanted to avoid it. I did witness one "incident" at Great Lakes and years later a recruit was killed there in the same kind of event that I'd witnessed. There's more to THAT story but I'll skip it. I assume that those things don't happen any more. I hope not anyway. And just as the author has led us to this place, he veers off to continue other story threads. This is a bit frustrating, but Mr. Jones DID create these other characters and stories and is therefore committed to continue them. Gotta stick with it. "only" 200+ dense pages to go. BTW, I'm upping my rating to 3.75 despite the over-writing(overwrought) problems.<br /><br />- More connections: "Catch-22," "M.A.S.H.," and that TV movie from the 1970 called "Tribes." And ... Denis Johnson's "Tree of Smoke" - like that book this one's uneven and struggles to be equal to(never mind greater than) the sum of its parts.<br /><br />- Ouch - there're a bunch of stories of suspicious recruit deaths at Great Lakes over the years. The guy I witnessed getting beaten up by two swim "instructors" survived at least. Sad ...<br /><br />- One of the G'reads reviewers suggest getting hold of the more recent edition of this book as it has the stuff in it that Jones' editors insisted was too graphic(gay sex). If that had been included here it might have given more weight and sense to what happened to Bloom. As for me I'm too far into this edition to begin again. The general issues of enlisted military life being one of whoring and boozing were certainly there when I was in the Navy(1965-69). However, Hawaii seemed to be much tamed down from what's depicted here. Two problems persisted, however. Military pay SUCKED back then and there always seemed to be a shortage of "normal" women around military bases in the USA. And so, in consequence, military bases drew homosexuals(with money) and prostitution. And LOTS of bars, of course. Kind of sick, actually. I wonder what it's like these days.<br /><br />Last night's reading focused on the Warden's romantic and Army administrative troubles, which seem to be spinning out of control. Poor Milt, he's lived his life and protected himself by succeeding as a controller and a manipulator, but lately seems to be getting the shaft in spite of all his prowess. Love and war will do that to ya I guess! Tonight ... back to the stockade drama.<br /><br />The end is coming, both in the story and in the lives of its protagonists. The end of their pre-war lives. I'll be finished soon, but probably not tonight. Gotta finish "Day of the Locust" first. Takes place in Los Angeles about 5 years earlier.<br /><br />Finished last night with a bit of skimming after Prewitt's fate is revealed. Same as the movie ... I lowered my rating back down to 3*(3.5*) after shuffling through several false endings. I've said enough about Mr. Jones' shortcomings as a writer. The book is VERY ambitious and JJ trys hard, too hard, in fact. One book that this reminds me of is Denis Johnson's "Tree of Smoke," another would-be epic that failed to be greater than the sum of its parts. Both books were WAY TOO LONG!</span>
  <a data-text-id="788524668918577258" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText2224906863976398578" style="display:none">Twenty pages in and wha? hey, already read this book; how many times have I done this (several times over the last ten years) but so rereads have been a thing w/me lately so read it again, ok. Well, lots of high style reading since last this book has produced a newer less-than appreciation for a novel that just tells it straight relying on plot/character and plenty of it to do the trick. Well, it don't, like it might have used to. This is a fine enough book that way, good descriptions of a way of life at a given time of history but short on artistry, so and so be content to read as such.<br /><br />Now, there are [almost always] some interesting aspects to be gleaned and pondered in every decent book if one looks for it - here it's the race/bigotry card question. Will books like this be next in line to statues being torn down across the land? It could happen and sooner than you think. Sad it will be to live in a hermetically sealed Pee See world. Orwell be damned an roll over it's here.</span>

<div id="review_327678908" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/327678908" />
    <a title="Aaron" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/99144-aaron"><img alt="Aaron" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1180146187p2/99144.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/327678908?book_show_action=true">May 10, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Aaron" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Aaron" href="/user/show/99144-aaron">Aaron</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer327678908" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11083334484716987364">This book is grim and dark, but also beautiful and wonderful. Each character is believable and understandable, and that makes some brutal events in the book have that much more impact. I mean that both in the sense that as a reader I sympathized with characters as events happened to them, but also in situations where I understood why a character acted out negatively or in a self-destructive way.<br/><br/>Jones' style can be a bit tricky to follow, especially when he launches into a long stream-of-consciou</span>
  <span id="freeText11083334484716987364" style="display:none">This book is grim and dark, but also beautiful and wonderful. Each character is believable and understandable, and that makes some brutal events in the book have that much more impact. I mean that both in the sense that as a reader I sympathized with characters as events happened to them, but also in situations where I understood why a character acted out negatively or in a self-destructive way.<br /><br />Jones' style can be a bit tricky to follow, especially when he launches into a long stream-of-consciousness paragraph, but it's so well done, and it got me into the characters' perspective so effectively that it was worth the effort for me. Having finished this yesterday, I know I'll carry the memory of these characters around for a long time - they feel so much like they were actual people.<br /><br />I can understand how <u>From Here to Eternity</u> may not have universal appeal, but for me, this was a magnificent read. I have an appreciation for and perspective of peacetime Army life that I didn't before. And for organizational politics. And for Hawaii. And for life in difficult circumstances. And for dudes who are too fatalistic for their own damn good. And maybe masculinity. And maybe people in general.<br /></span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_350380659" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/350380659" />
    <a title="Richard" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1751300-richard"><img alt="Richard" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1228958396p2/1751300.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/350380659?book_show_action=true">Jun 17, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Richard" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Richard" href="/user/show/1751300-richard">Richard</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780440327707" href="/book/show/1808345.From_Here_to_Eternity">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer350380659" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10157561526530070375">This is an epic-sized book of almost nine hundred pages which takes place in the late-1941 months preceding the Japanese attack on Pearl Harbor. Despite the book's size, I don't remember ever being tired of the thing. It is totally engrossing, due mainly to the indelible characters who populate it. <br/><br/>The main character is Private Robert E. Lee Prewitt, an ex-bugler who has just been assigned to Company G of an infantry regiment stationed at Schofield Barracks on Oahu. He did extracurricular servic</span>
  <span id="freeText10157561526530070375" style="display:none">This is an epic-sized book of almost nine hundred pages which takes place in the late-1941 months preceding the Japanese attack on Pearl Harbor. Despite the book's size, I don't remember ever being tired of the thing. It is totally engrossing, due mainly to the indelible characters who populate it. <br /><br />The main character is Private Robert E. Lee Prewitt, an ex-bugler who has just been assigned to Company G of an infantry regiment stationed at Schofield Barracks on Oahu. He did extracurricular service as a boxer in his old assignment. Continuing in this activity in his new unit would have placed him in favored status with the competition-conscious peacetime army officers, and would have rewarded him with lighter duty assignments as a perk for upholding his company's macho image. He is fully expected to participate in boxing at Schofield, but he refuses to join the company boxing team because he had blinded a sparring partner in the ring and he was haunted by the damage he inflicted on the unfortunate soldier, who was a good friend of Pruitt's. <br /><br />Prewitt therefore starts fairly early as a pariah at his new posting. He is given "the treatment" by the company's sergeants after he makes it clear he will not change his mind about boxing. He is literally run into the ground physically in order to reform his stubbornness. The only person in authority who treats him humanely is the company First Sergeant, Milt Warden, who must maintain a facade of official non concern in Prewitt's hazing but respects his solid soldier skills. Prewitt's only friend is a trouble-prone private named Maggio. <br /><br />Many story elements unfold until the book's climactic scenes occur against the backdrop of the Pearl Harbor attack. Jones creates a cruel, cynical world in which the army's spit and polish overlays a soldier life spent in e.m. clubs, cat houses, even military stockades. The Hawaii the men inhabit when they get off-post is hardly a tourist paradise. A foul smelling institutional torpor runs their lives, personified by G Company's C.O., Captain Holmes, who resents the short time he must spend each day away from the polo field in order to visit the company to make sure First Sergeant Warden is keeping his ass covered. People on all military levels try to survive while cataclysmic events unfold. The ability of Jones to maintain the tension to the end of the book is evidence of his greatness as an author. He won the National Book Award for this book in 1952, and the book was adapted for the classic, award-winning 1953 film of the same title.<br /><br /><br />James Jones served with the 25th Infantry Division at Schofield Barracks in Oahu, where he witnessed the Japanese attack on the Army post and other American installations on December 7th, 1941. He fought on Guadalcanal, where he received a wound which led to his return to the United States for recuperation. He had enough wartime experiences to provide the basis for several books. "From Here to Eternity" is the first title of Jones' "War Trilogy." "The Thin Red Line", released about ten years later, tells the experience of an army infantry unit trying to survive the death trap of Guadalcanal. "Whistle", released after Jones' death in 1977, relates the story of G.I.'s sent back home on a hospital ship, trying to recuperate at a stateside hospital while having trouble coping with their wartime memories.<br /><br />You can look for the characters in "From Here to Eternity" in the second and third books in the trilogy, but you won't find them, at least not literally. I was surprised Sgt. Warden and the rest of his platoon weren't in "The Thin Red Line" when I read it. Actually, they are there, in spirit. I found out in Wikipedia that Jones carried the central characters of the first book over to the next two, under different names. Thus, for example, there is a marked similarity intentionally built into the characters of Warden and Prewitt of "From Here to Eternity", Welsh and Witt in "The Thin Red Line" and Winch and Prell in "Whistle." Maintaining the same traits, but changing the outward identity allowed Jones the freedom to experience the whole trilogy as the essential characters reacted to unfolding tragedy, even though some characters may not have survived the whole time frame.<br /><br />Censorship was still active when this book was first published, and publishers sometimes self-censored books to head off problems in getting them distributed. Jones had a dispute with his publisher about the deletion of some (but not all) four-letter words and some depictions of gay sex. Purists who like to read a book exactly the way the author intended can now access the Kindle edition of "From Here to Eternity" thanks to Jones' daughter, Kaylie Jones, who arranged to have the book reissued in unexpurgated digital form in 2011.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10157561526530070375" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('RZZhexwm7iMBamfEvtBfTMi7VOeVXfUHval7cCQB3pr5FhFXOGV/eg15AyrPD8IoaKqXf1dEc7k2FMuovocCeQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('IsXVoY67vsVTfhKE251aMFG43C6hv0/44Rv2pAqUvmSeRaWNqvgvnF9tdmqqQsdU8akftmOmyUZqpkZ8kBJihw==')})
  }

Formerly a governess, she has<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText12172535823386282071\" style=\"display:none\">One woman\'s beauty fells the whole of Oxford in this sidesplitting classic campus novel.Nobody could predict the consequences when ravishing Zuleika Dobson arrives at Oxford, to visit her grandfather, the college warden. Formerly a governess, she has landed on the occupation of prestidigitator, and thanks to her overwhelming beauty—and to a lesser extent her professional talents—she takes the town by storm, gaining admittance to her grandfather\'s college. It is there, at the institution inspired by Beerbohm\'s own alma mater, that she falls in love with the Duke of Dorset, who duly adores her in return. The protagonist of Booth Tarkington\'s great historical drama is George Amberson Minafer, the spoiled and arrogant grandson of the founder of the family\'s magnificence. Eclipsed by a new breed of developers, financiers, and manufacturers, this pampered scion begins his gradual descent from the midwestern aristocracy to the working class. Today The Magnificent Ambersons is best known through the 1942 Orson Welles movie, but as the critic Stanley Kauffmann noted, &quot;It is high time that [the novel] appear again, to stand outside the force of Welles\'s genius, confident in its own right.&quot; &quot;The Magnificent Ambersons is perhaps Tarkington\'s best novel,&quot; judged Van Wyck Brooks. &quot;[It is] a typical story of an American family and town--the great family that locally ruled the roost and vanished virtually in a day as the town spread and darkened into a city. This novel no doubt was a permanent page in the social history of the United States, so admirably conceived and written was the tale of the Amber-sons, their house, their fate and the growth of the community in which they were submerged in the end.&quot;\n\nBooth Tarkington (1869-1946), a prolific writer who achieved overnight success with his first novel, The Gentleman from Indiana (1899), is perhaps best remembered as the author of the popular Penrod adventures and Seventeen (1916). <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer4831969126792743454"><b>James Ramon Jones</b> was an American author known for his explorations of World War II and its aftermath.<br/><br/>His wartime experiences inspired some of his most famous works. He witnessed the Japanese attack on Pearl Harbor, which led to his first published novel, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/116114' rel='nofollow noopener'>From Here to Eternity</a>. <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/92417' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Thin Red Line</a> reflected his combat experiences on Guadalcanal. His last novel, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/144908' rel='nofollow noopener'>Whistle</a>, was based on his hospital sta</span>
  <span id="freeText4831969126792743454" style="display:none"><b>James Ramon Jones</b> was an American author known for his explorations of World War II and its aftermath.<br /><br />His wartime experiences inspired some of his most famous works. He witnessed the Japanese attack on Pearl Harbor, which led to his first published novel, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/116114" rel="nofollow noopener">From Here to Eternity</a>. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/92417" rel="nofollow noopener">The Thin Red Line</a> reflected his combat experiences on Guadalcanal. His last novel, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/144908" rel="nofollow noopener">Whistle</a>, was based on his hospital stay in Memphis, Tennessee, recovering from his wounds.<br /><br /><i>Excerpted from <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/James_Jones_(author)" rel="nofollow noopener">Wikipedia</a>.</i></span>

Whe<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText9482492011956105524\" style=\"display:none\">&quot;When compared to the fact that he might very well be dead by this time tomorrow, whether he was courageous or not today was pointless, empty. When compared to the fact that he might be dead tomorrow, everything was pointless. Life was pointless. Whether he looked at a tree or not was pointless. It just didn\'t make any difference. It was pointless to the tree, it was pointless to every man in his outfit, pointless to everybody in the whole world. Who cared? It was not pointless only to him; and when he was dead, when he ceased to exist, it would be pointless to him too. More important: Not only would it be pointless, it would have been pointless, all along.&quot;\n\nSuch is the ultimate significance of war in The Thin Red Line (1962), James Jones\'s fictional account of the battle between American and Japanese troops on the island of Guadalcanal. The narrative shifts effortlessly among multiple viewpoints within C-for-Charlie Company, from commanding officer Capt. James Stein, his psychotic first sergeant Eddie Welsh, and the young privates they send into battle. The descriptions of combat conditions—and the mental states it induces—are unflinchingly realistic, including the dialog (in which a certain word Norman Mailer rendered as &quot;fug&quot; 15 years earlier in The Naked and the Dead appears properly spelled on numerous occasions). This is more than a classic of combat fiction; it is one of the most significant explorations of male identity in American literature, establishing Jones as a novelist of the caliber of Herman
